All these people in the comments talking about arcing with 12V...SMH.  There's a reason things that actually make use of electrical arcs are always very high voltage, even for short arcs. You aren't arcing _shit_ with 12V. The only thing that *could* be happening is intermittent/poor electrical contact, which would very greatly reduce (or eliminate) the amount of current traveling that path and cause the current load to shift to the rest of the available conductor, while increasing the overall resistance of the connector/adapter/cable system delivering power to the GPU.   Higher resistance in the adapter equals higher power dissipation in the adapter. There are no arcs involved. Just higher system resistance.

Its harness engineering 101... NEVER EVER terminate wires with solder that are subject to motion.  Crimped connections are the only reliable termination for harness wires.  And if for some reason solder is the only termination that works you better have a robust strain relief that completely isolations the solder joint from mechanical forces.

Counterintuitively you wouldn't expect to see burning where the wires are disconnected, because a higher resistance leads to lower current, and you'd expect the melting to occur on the plate instead of the pins (since it is where the majority of current density is located). This seems more like a physical pin connection issue, where the pins that don't burn up are in fact the ones not making a full connection.

Building on this: I think the outer wires having a single pin is the issue, unless there is some design feature where power is biased to the double pins. The outer pins have a higher resistance which creates more heat, weakening the metal contact, burning the plastic, and ultimately causing a pin to fail. This leads to a worsening of the issue on the opposite single pin, which also quickly leads to it failing (and even more melting on that side). The bending radius could be a contributing factor to the pin failing, but this is just a poor design overall.

When putting devices in parallel, the current will naturally follow the path of least resistance, and even a few milli-ohms can make a difference when you are sending AMPs through the connection. The bus bar at the connector end is an attempt to alleviate this issue. One approach to a test would be to get an adapter, wiggle it back and forth a few times left to right, then measure the resistance across the outer connector to the other end of the wire with a multimeter. As others have said below, if the wire is barely connected by a few threads / has a high resistance, then the current traveling across the wire will generate heat, more resistance equals more heat.The heat then travels nearby to the rest of the connector or along the wire and spreads out damaging the connector / wire. If it's disconnected completley then there's no resistance across that wire, but the other wires have to take more current which overall results in less heat, but more load than they should be taking.  It's something I've seen happen with mains powered heaters wired into dodgy mains sockets. Imagine water as water preasure, current is water speed and greater resistance is a smaller pipe.
If you want to recreate the failure read further! You won‚Äôt be able to recreate the failure with such a massive wires going into the ‚Äúadapter‚Äù they are probably capable of handling the current even 2 are disconnected. The problem is with the actual metallic female pin inside the connector! Here‚Äôs why: they become loose (the actual hole is enlarged) and will not create enough contact surface with the male pin on the actual card plug. Smaller contact surface will create heat and will result in a melted connector. The design of the adapter is bad because it joins together 2 pins making them stiff enough to make them difficult to get centered when they are inserted into the card connector. Nvidia did a great job by using smaller gauge cables on individual pins, this allows each female pin to get centered on the male pin when connector is inserted. Also this design is better for cable twisting, will also prevent the enlargement of the female pin. If you want to recreate the failure, just take a small screwdriver and enlarge a few of the female pins‚Ä¶

If it's the outermost pins getting hotter, it doesn't make any sense to say that it is due to the outermost wires getting lose or the solder joints cracking. Instead it depends by the way you insert and remove the connector. If you angle it sideways a lot the tiny pinchers inside the female connector get deformed. This explains why it's the side ones being problematic. The deformation renders the fit of the female on the male a bit loose reducing the area of contact hence the area where the power flows through. This is proved by the ATX standards stating that (I don't remember the exact number) the estimate life cycle of the connectors are like 20 insertions. If you connect it ONLY ONCE and PERFECTLY STRAIGHT in nothing is never going to happen.

I would think it's not the extra draw from the center wires but arcing caused by the break still being in close proximity to it's original location that's causing the heat. Good Video thank you! Some criticism if I may; terminology matters! Please be careful when making statements like 'Due to high resistance the cable will burn' This is contrary to Ohm's Law; V=IR.  High resistance = low current = low heat!!! You get heat, when your current goes up- not the other way around.  When you are looking at the issue you must think in terms of Ohm's law.  The other part of the equation is how the cable design works; say they are distributing the power across 4 wires.  SO from a circuit standpoint you have 4 parallel paths current is lower, than if you start breaking off paths, current across the remaining wires goes up to compensate for the overall power draw- thats when you will see the heat.  From the picture at 4:55, the contacts are not evenly distributed; the inner wires get 2 contacts per wire, while the outer is only 1. so you would have roughly double the current through the outer contacts relative to the inner ones (which would make them hotter)  So if in your test you used one of the connectors routed to the inner pins you wouldnt see the issue.  Make sure you track to a pigtail to the outer pins then retry your wiggle test.

It's not necessarily the fact the connection was lost, but the "arcing" that happens when current is passing through a connection that has increased resistance...such as a break in the solder.  There could still be enough connectivity to get "some" current through it, but the higher resistance of the broken connection causes the temp to increase. If you've ever worked with wiring in your home,  you will immediately recognize what I'm talking about.  That arcing from just bringing two contacts with a difference in potential close enough together that they can pass current just before they physically touch.  That arc contains a LOT of heat and repeated instances of that arcing continues to build the heat....a continuous cycle until failure.
Time to start liquid cooling your wires

A solution would be to split the wires half way up the cable and crimp instead of solder. Then individually pin each wire to the connector.
I have to argue that Igor's assessment is incorrect. If the detached wire is causing increased current to that pin of the plug via the middle wires, the failure would occur in the "foil" bridge between the outer and inner wires. This is thinner than the pin, so will heat up faster under the increased load. The fact these images show the melting is occurring within the plug/socket connection leads me to the opinion that the fit of the pin in the socket is more the problem, which would also be exaggerated when the wire is bent near the plug. If the pin/socket connection is loose to begin, then bending the wire at a tight angle would result in 1 or 2 single points of contact instead of the length of the pin. This increases current draw, therefore creating heat. If this is more of a problem after the cable has been plugged/unplugged a few times, then it is possible this is what is causing the socket to expand. I've seen the same issue with the old IDE HDD power leads. After a plugging/unplugging a few times, the sockets would be stretched enough that a poor connection resulted. Squash the socket closed a little and all was good again. To test this, try expanding one of the sockets a little and test again. Sorry for the long post.

before i watch, i wanted to add on previous vid: Electrons "flow"(or hole flow..........let's not go there) on the surface. Ever see MIG gnd wire? Hair thin wires,  hundreds. That's because the surface area of 100 hair thin wires is greater than a single equal diameter wire(*true story).  But ultimately! when a contact is made and broken, current surges. You can see this on any cheap o-scope. Each tiny "pit" causes resistance/impedance. But! Heat is generated in the place of said resistance. This causes a closed loop spiral. End result: Melted. *Iyes i'm an en gy near lol. But this is basic EE, no degree needed. You are not recreating it because you are performing the right experiment. It's the arcing that is causing the melting. when you cut the cables and don't put the cables close to each other to create arcing there is obviously no heat created. Electrical arc produces heat at a rate that is equal to the voltage x the current of the arc. The equation used for this is called "incident energy" "arc flash" and you could calculate this knowing the specs of your system etc.

I learned the hard way as a young engineer about compression pin fretting corrosion and high power interfaces. When high pulsed currents pass through a low compression interface heating causes the metal to expand and contract. This results in micro movements between the male and female parts breaking the tin oxide barrier and allowing oxygen to interact with the base metal and form microscopic corrosion. This increases resistance which in turn increases heat in turn increasing the fretting action and eventually thermal runaway melting your connection. This effect can also occur with even micro vibration between surfaces.
Molex in 1960: "Hey guys here is a new type of connector, you don't solder it you just crimp it, it's much more solid and it doesn't crack like solder!" I am a mechanic by trade and a computer enthusiast as a hobby. I have seen these types of failures in heavy equipment many times. I think there might be a step that is being missed in these videos. The tests done by Steve and Jay have had the strain relief removed during testing which can hide a phenomenon where a solder joint will break but the wire will still be contacting the mating surface by the force of the tape holding it down creating an insane amount of resistance. If a wire was broken but still being held on the pad out could generate the type of heat necessary to melt the connector. It might be worth trying to break or desolder the wire and then rewrap it to give it just enough contact for electricity to pass through with a very high resistance and see what happens.
after seeing egor plug i see whats going on when it brakes with that much power its causing a ark melting the plasic
If the plug is heating enough to melt, it's going to make the solder joints look bad. It's basically heating the solder enough to soften it and cause a cold solder joint. It's resoldering itself as a cold joint.
Jay, you talk about increased current on the central wires, after the outer wires are compromised (which does happen to some degree)... but technically, there must be substantial current flowing through the outer pins and wires to cause the heating in those locations.  It is a matter of increased resistance in those poor connections - not actual breaks which would result in zero current.  The central pins are doubled up for their incoming wires, soldered together, so they heat up less because they share current on twice the contact surface.  They do have pretty minimal strength in those soldered pins and "foil" contacts.

Just a speculation from an electrician on why you couldn't recreate it.  Completely removing the connection doesn't always bring temps up higher when ran in parallel.  A lot of times when you see the higher temps it's because of a loose connection adding more resistance.  Yes the other wires will get slightly hotter, but you are loosing that extra resistance from the loose connection, thus heat being generated.  Once again, just a speculation. Edit: Just to clarify, I'm talking about the "outer pins are melting" situation specifically. Some statements will remain true in any case, but that should be noted.  I'm not done watching yet (got to 6:53 so far), but I don't think the outer cables break off entirely and all the power gets drawn through the two middle ones (or those and one on the side). When a connection gets damaged but not entirely disconnected, its resistance increases, so now power will dissipate as heat there instead of where it's supposed to go. On the pictures it looks to me that the pins are one continous piece from the card end right up to the tab the wire is soldered to. The pins are then interconnected via the foil, but the thermal interface will naturally be worse across there. The outer cables appear to be connected primarily, in some units possibly only, to the outer pin tabs. Even when connected to the next tab in line, the physical connection will be weaker and could easily be broken while the outer tab and cable remain connected despite damage. So only the outer pins have bad connections where extra heat is generated. The heat will be distributed via the foil, but since pin and foil are two separate pieces of metal with an imperfect thermal interface, the outer pins will be hotter than the rest. If the connections of the outer cables were completely severed, the pins in the middle would melt first - or the cables/the connectors at the other side of the cables.

It's pretty insane that they're using soldered joints at all for those current levels. For house electricity, you're not *allowed* to use solder joints for any high current application because soldered joint will receive the heat first and if that solder ever melts, you've major issues. Always used grimped connections for any high current application and you'll be fine.  And since solder is usually also weaker metal than the wire, any force will cause cracking in the solder first and with a crack you will get sparking and high temps very fast, which again can melt the solder (or burn the surrounding material).
Little remark: There is no such thing as resistance of power. The material has the resistance and sending too much power through a higher resistance (now much less material to get the power through after the break) is what causes the heat. The now shitty contact on the breakage point also ends up with a higher resistance and you could end up with arcing which doesn't help either.

Jay, what you're saying makes no sense.  If the problem was that the outside connections were broken and all that power was forced through the middle wires, the middle connections would be the ones that melted, not the outside ones.  I'll tell you EXACTLY what the problem is and why the outside connectors are melted even though they're the ones that had the connection broken.  If no current was running through the outside connectors, the outside connectors wouldn't be melted.  It looks to me like that connecion was broken but not broken enough.  The two pieces of metal were still close enough for the current to occasionally arc.  When current arcs, it passes through the air.  The problem is that air is an insulator and there's tremendous friction when electricity forces its way through it.  The friction is so great that the air turns white-hot (which is what we see when lightning strikes).  The heat generated by the arcing current is what melted the plug.  That's why the outside connectiors are both the ones with the broken connections AND the melted plastic.

The busbar used is a great solution where the strain on a cable is minimal. If it breaks then a couple of things can happen. If the outside cables/solder joint/pin is broken and no longer able to transfer power, the remaining connections can overload. The heat can melt a connector. The second thing that can happen is if the wire can still handle power, a broken pin or loose connection can cause sparking across the air gap , continual sparking can cause connectors to melt. Poorly soldered connectors on the main board could also cause similar issues. Your interpretation is incorrect, about the cause being the soldering. I have vast knowledge of high current connections and can tell you with 100% confidence that the black burning is arcing. It can only occur on a loose connection or one that is broken with a very small distance for the arcing to occur. If the connection is solid, arcing cannot happen. bending the wires close to the connection will cause arcing. You need to test this over hours, not a few minutes. Arcing might not occur for a good while after installation as it could work a little loose and then cause the problem.

Everybody is wrong... I think. The overheating looks like it is occurring at the end of the connector where the plug makes contact with the jack.  I think it is more likely the issue that was detailed by PCI-SIG. Why? 1. The melting isn't occurring at the foil 2. In a multi-contact current path, breaking a single path or a flakey single path increases the resistance which means the current increases along the other paths.  That means the inner conductors would need to break for the current to increase the current to the outer current. I doubt the inner conductors are breaking on  that foil given their mechanical position. 3. The molding looks like it is acting as a strain relief. It looks like most of the stress is being seen by the molding AND most importantly being seen by the joint between the jack and plug where the actual melting is occurring 4. It's definitely not arcing. That requires a large voltage delta which only occurs when there is a break between all 12V connectors to the foil.  If conductor A breaks, conductors B, C, .. all keep the voltage roughly at the same level on the foil as conductor A. This isn't a situation most of us are used to when you take car battery leads and tap them against the battery terminals and see sparks.  In that case there is only one point of contact and you are making and breaking it. My opinion: it is a strain issue with bending but the overheating is at the jack and plug connection points which is where the melting is occurring. I think all the conductors are not making good contact at these joints in the bad situation.  The outer conductors are making the best contact for some reason which means most of the current goes through these contacts then it overheats and melts

It must be arcing! Ummm no. We can explain how these particular pins are heating up using Ohm's Law.  R = V/I, I = P/V or I = V/R  Power consumed can be calculated with: P = V/I or P = IIR or P = (VV)/R  For 600W power draw, the current is 600/12 = 50A. We have 6 x 12v pins on the 12 pin adapter so each pin is carrying 50/6 = 8.33333333A. For 12v to push through 50A the total resistance across the GPU 12v rail is 12/50 = 0.24 ohms. Given we have 8.33A per 12V pin the resistance per pin calculated using R = V/I is 12 / 8.33333333 = 1.44 ohms.  The pins are in parallel so we can check this by calculating the parallel resistance using 1/Req = 1/R1 + 1/R2 ... 1/R6. 1/1.44 + 1/1.44 + 1/1.44 + 1/1.44 + 1/1.44 + 1/1.44 = 4.166666667 = 1/Req Req = 1 / 4.166666667 Req = 0.24 ohms  Note Ohm's law is always true so as power draw decreases, the total resistance seen at the GPU 12 pin connector increases dynamically. The opposite is also true, as power draw increases, the total resistance seen at the GPU 12 pin connector decreases dynamically.   Now Ohm's laws also states that voltage drops proportionally across resistors in series, with each resistor dissipating the proportional amount of power. For example, if we have 12v across a resistive load of 2 x 1K ohm resistors, the total resistance is 2K ohm and using I = V/R, we are drawing 12/2,000 = 6mA.  Next let's calculate the voltage drop across each resistor using V = IR. For R1 0.006 x 1000 = 6V For R2 0.006 x 1000 = 6V  Now calculate the power in Watts dissipated by each resistor using P = VI. For R1 6 x 0.006 = 0.036W For R2 6 x 0.006 = 0.036W  Total = 0.072W (72 mW)  Let's check this by calculating the total power draw for the circuit using the law P = IIR (commonly called I squared R). 0.006^2 x 2000 = 0.072W   So what is happening in this connector? When an outside solder joint is broken, resistance is introduced into the joint and let's assume this is 0.2 ohms. This cracked joint resistance is in series with the pin's resistance at the card connector. Now let's do our Ohm's Law math and consider this new resistance.  As per the above calculations the pin resistance is 1.44 ohms. Total resistance including the cracked joint is 1.44 + 0.2 = 1.64 ohms. Total current draw is 12 / 1.46 = 7.317073171 The power dissipation across the cracked joint can be calculated using P = IIR 7.317073171^2 * 0.2 = 10.707911957 Watts.  10.71 Watts dissipated by a pin with no cooling! The pin is dissipating so much power it is heating up and baking.  The whole PCIe 5.0 12VHPWR cable spec is utter madness. I honestly don't understand why 12v power to modern GPU's is not delivered using a single pair of 6mm 50A power cables with crimped terminals and Anderson type connectors.
What i don't get as an electrical engineer we solved this problem in like the 70's. Higher power requires thicker wires and radial connectors. This looks like me as form over functionality and as an engineer it makes me sad.

I wouldn't have a solder joint on a cable like this. I bodged together a work light using an LED lightbar for a car and a 12V PSU in a weatherproof box, using the classic XT60 connectors for the cabling, including an extension cable. These XT60 connectors are soldered like these adapters, however, they solder into a cup on the back of the contact in the connector weather than a flat plate. I'm crap at soldering them so they routinely break. I got tired of repairing them and switched to Anderson Powerpole connectors which have contacts that crimp to the wire instead of being soldered and I've not had to repair them since.  I feel like the soldering of the wires to the connector is a major weak point, they should be crimped somehow IMO.

What NVIDIA has done wrong: 1.Never solder a wire terminal unless there is a solid base or an interface (like a PCB).  The copper becomes brittle and it can break easily if bent 2-3 times, especially if thin sheets are used like in this case. 2.The way they choose the power rating using that extra pin header is a bit gimmicky. There is not an "active" way of determining the power rating of the PSU or the quality of the connection. 3.There is no input voltage monitoring. Assuming the GPU is drawing a constant power (~300W). Excluding the efficiency, let's say at 12V, 25A are needed. If the voltage on the  12V line drops to let's say 10V at the input of the card due to bad contacts/interrupted traces, to deliver the same power, 30A are required this time. Basically, it's a runaway situation: the connector heats up, the voltage drop increases, the drawn current increases. So, by monitoring the input voltage, this runaway situation could have been prevented. VRMs do their best to deliver the same power required by the GPU.  4. The connector itself looks cheaper than a typ. PCI-E connector. At this price point they could have done a more elegant solution having 2 PCBs: one dedicated for typ. PCI-E power connectors (a small one) interfaced with the main PCB; or a totally new connector (similar to XT60).

nope wrong, and that is not how electricity works. its the crimps. the female part has a slit interface and if u bend it a bit and u get a to low contact pressure and that will create a hot spot. this hot spot will melt the plastic.  but how can removing half of the wires melt the pins? halving of the wires will make them 4 times as hot. but the wires are not melting. if the heat is generated by the bridge inside how com it melts at a different spot and not the hot spot?  i have American and European certificates for crimping connectors. im also IPC certificates in soldering.  i make tester and test equipment for PCBA and final products. as u know connectors have a maximum number of cycles. we exceed the numbers on the daily because most crimps don't have a high wear resistend model. testing 10.000 units with a internal proprietary connector with a 5 time plug and unplug limit will make u cry. making a connector that fits with crimp that can handle 500 to 5000 cycles is one of the things i do.  the crimp are bent open just a bit so that the contact point is very small. this make a intenser hot spot that melts the plastic. al they need to do is use high spec crimt that will stay in the correct shape after the have bin bent to the limit the plastic housing to allows. make the space the crimp contacts can move smaller or make the crimp out of minutely thicker material and have the fingers spring back without permanently deforming. a different finger design may also work.  just today i replaced machined crimps that cost 5.50‚Ç¨ apiece in a clean-room for ASML testing rigs. that connect to our in-house developed test machinery. it can see the differences between a B40 and a C40 type circuit breaker by the resisters ramp-up because of the current.  i know connectors.

I have experience with soldering going back to the 80s and if cables are made where stress relief does NOT exist, then it will fail. You cannot design a cable to take stress where it depends on the solder itself to take the stress.  Back in the 80s we were taught to wrap the wire or crimp it then solder so that the stresses of the wire being pulled don't (over time) fracture the solder and break.  I was very surprised to see the wires just laying on top of a solder plane. That's a bad design.

I'm not convinced.  By this theory, the outside wires are the ones that break, and if that's the case then the outside conductors aren't the conductors drawing the excess current --- so it should be the center conductors drawing extra current.  That would imply that the inner plastic keyguides would be the ones first to melt, but it's the outside keyguides that melt first.       But that doesn't make sense for another reason.  The inner conductors are already drawing half the current of the outside conductors by design.  There's no way that breaking an outer conductor would increase the current on the inner conductors sufficiently to cause a problem.      IMO opinion it's going to end up being resistive heating of the OUTER two contacts.      The way they built this connector, the inner four contacts are being used in pairs, but the outer two contacts are being used singly.      This contact is being used to draw 50 AMPS of current.  Over the four wires, that's 12.5 amps per line.  The inner two lines are transmitting those 12.5 amps across two pins each, or just 6.25 amps per pin --- but the outer two contacts are passing 12.5 amps each.      Resistive heating goes as I\^2\*R, and the contacts are rated at .005 ohms.  For the inner contacts At 6.25 amps, you get (6.25 amps)\^2 \* .005 ohms = 195 milliwatts of heat per contact --- and that's not too bad, even if they are all right next to each other.      But for the OUTER contacts, you have (12.5 amps)\^2 \* .005 ohms = 781 milliwatts of heat per pin!  And for a pin with no heat sink completely surrounded by plastic, that's actually a lot of heat!   Now let's add it all up --- 781 mw \* 2 + 195 \* 4 = 2.3 watts * 2 directions (power+ground) = 4.6 W of resistive heating when working *correctly* inside a small connector that is surrounded by plastic and has no effective cooling.    It's enough heat that I'd be worried about that connector even if it was exposed to nothing more than high ambient temperatures.      But suppose now that the connector isn't quite fully inserted, or is slightly oxidized, or has had too many insertion cycles applied, or the contact is bent.      If the effective connector resistance rises even slightly, the heat produced will rise linearly.  And the contact resistance is currently just .005 ohms.  If some small thing is causing the contact resistance to rise to, say .01 ohms, it's going to double the heat output to 10 watts.      Now suppose that it's the INNER contacts that fail, rather than the outer ones as proposed.  In THAT scenario, the current passing through the remaining three input wires rises to 50 amps / 2 leads = 25 amps!      Now on the outer wires we have (25 amps)\^2 \* .005 ohms = 3.12 watts PER OUTER CONTACT!  * 4 contacts = 12.5W and that is certainly enough heat to melt the plastic.      I strongly expect this is going to come down to two things: high case temperatures combined with an ever-so-slight increase in contact resistance or failure of the inner contacts.      If you want to force a failure, cut one to four wires going to an INNER pair of conductors on purpose to raise the current on the two OUTER conductors.  Then raise the ambient temperature close to what you find in a case and don't remove the insulating boot from the connector.  I'm pretty sure you'll see it melt.  Don't open it to cut the lines, leave it closed and thermally insulated.

That's one of the "fun" things about electrical current. Most thermal "failures" are due to build quality. With a poor quality connector you can easily find yourself in this scenario. Current generates heat. At some point, excessive heat will lower the resistance enough to allow more current, which generates more heat, which lowers the resistance more, which generates more current...you see where I'm going? Power draw isn't the problem. It's the amps going through the wire that causes melting. That's why you can't send 20 amps through a 22 gauge wire. There's not enough wire to dissipate the heat. By breaking the connectors, you're essentially forcing the same amperage to flow across half the wires, which will generate more heat, and lower resistance.... And you know where I'm going with this.

-----WARNING-----  THIS SAME ISSUE occurs on crappy molex to SATA power adapters on the SATA side.  In every instance I have seen online, the SATA adapters that fail are these over-molded connectors.  And the best part?  It doesn't matter if the wires are soldered to a board or if they are soldered onto individual pins and THEN put into a molding machine to create the plastic/rubber around the wires.  The issue is the over-molding.  It allows cheap chinese manufacturers to crank these things out with no regard to QC, because the consumer doesn't have access to an X-ray machine to non-destructively check the adapter for these issues.  As long as they use these connectors: --If a solder joint breaks, it will melt. --If solder ALMOST bridges 2 wires, it will melt. --If you solder the wires onto little pins to insert into a pre-made connector,  and  If any of those pins shifted when you injection mold on the cable's strain relief,  IT WILL MELT.  These adapters are cheap as hell because injection molding plastic over shoddy workmanship is cheaper than a QC department.

If a connector is stressed to cause a poor connection it will arc current. The arcing will cause heat which melts the connector and if hot enough can also melt the solder. Once pins become open the total current will increase across the remaining connected pins. It would be expected that both the socket and connector would experience damage. Overall a poor design when multiple connectors are required for safe operation over the life of card vs the warranty period.
@jayztwocents the issue is, that a crack or a partial break will result in resistance increase and can also cause arcs which also increase heat , best case scenario is a total break

fact is, it's not just "clean breaks".  Yes, shooting that kind of current though only 2 wires vs 4 will do exactly what Igor states...but what is most likely happening is this combined with arcing.  That's going to cause temps to soar.    As for sourcing?  More than likely Shenzhen.  Yes, with stuff like thism, quality depends on who filled the seat that day.  That design is sketch at best, and the over-molding they did was to act like a strain relief...which obviously didn't work.  The math works for the design obviously, the 14ga wire can handle 15A each, at 12V that means each wire can handle 180W so 4x180 = 720W safely (480W card), they actually could have gotten away with 16Ga wire that has a 13A rating so it would be 624Watts max which is right about the 125% over-rating many places use for design, so it could actually be argued that NVIDA over-spec'd the wiring.    In the end, the design of that cable end is highly suspect, they attempted to prevent breakage with the over-molding of the wires.  Honestly, they would have been better off just using an 8 pin Molex instead of that 12, and looking into board layout and trace demand.  The biggest reason to split it 8 ways is most likely due to PCB requirements (not enough real-estate to handle high capacity traces on the board).  Still good stuff Jay, and Igor isn't wrong here, but it's probably a combination of arcing (which generates a whole lot more heat than if the wire just wasn't there).

im not an electrician. i worked for a company in uk that had to renovate a house that had had fire damage. the cause of the fire damage was the cable from socket to socket should of been one cable but it was 2 cables joined in the middle. due to work on one of the sockets the cable was pulled and the join of twisted cables together pulled apart and that caused an arc of electricity that set fire to the insulation.  when one of those cracks in the solder appear, it can cause arcing and that could be what its causing the extra heat to melt the plugs. BIG YIKS
@JayzTwoCents Having worked for a company that created test PCB's for electronics company prototypes, plus another company soldering Geophone strings for the oil industry I learned the soldering quality is VITAL. For the 1st company, My task was to hand solder the few components that couldn't be wave soldered (passed through a solder bath with a standing wave). Every solder joint on these PCB's was then examined under a microsope and also an X-Ray microsope to check for the amount of solder plus dry joints, cracks or bubbles within the solder. ANY defect meant the PCB came back to me to be reworked, i.e. removing any excess and applying additional flux and remelting the solder, or, adding flux and solder if there wasn't enough. The 1st set of 20 boards had 17 defects, the 2nd set had 5, after that zero defects.  For the 2nd I was tasked with winding the terminal wires onto a stress ring plus twisting together and soldering the power connections and tinning the sensor wires for connection to the geophones. EVERY string made had a sign off sheet for every process, once completed they were ALL tested to 200% capacity and also vibration stress tested, any failure could therefore be tracked back to the oprerator and remedial work carried out re-enforcing the quality standard required. I had 3 strings come back to me in the 1st week and then zero whilst maintaining the highest production rate out of 50 people doing the same task. Both jobs every joint I soldered was tested beyond the standard operating levels expected.   In cable manufacturing speed is the holy grail, quality is secondary, they test for defects and "Does it work?" and that's as far as it goes. Watching your vid and looking at Igors images highlights this, on your connector the solder surface is smooth and you can still see the wire strands just under the solder surface on the 1st 2nd and 3rd joints as the correct amount of solder has been applied plus, the wires are all flat on the contact pad, meaning the operator has held the wire until the solder has fully solidified.  The 4th the wire has lifted from the pad as the operator has reduced their pressure just as the solder has solidified, at worst this can be a stress multiplier like a lever if bent by the user. On all 4 wires the solder hasn't wicked up under the insulation and you can see the individual strands are clean, enough heat applied and the right amount of solder used, the wires can flex almost right up to the connector. Nice workmanship but distracted at the final step because they're already shifting focus to the next connector.  On Igors connector the solder surface is lumpy, you cannot see any strands because too much solder has been applied and the operator has been moving the wire as the solder solidifies, the worst being the 3rd, which looks like the iron was removed before the solder fully melted or not enough flux was applied. Too much heat has been applied and for too long, allowing the solder to wick along the strands and under the insulation, creating a longer lever and transferring more stress to the neck of the pin. Moving the wires during the "Freeze" of the solder can cause cracks and voids increasing resistance through the joint leading to increased heat. Shoddy workmanship throughout but good enough to pass the "Does it work?" Q/C level.  If the wires are bent vertically, up or down and being pressed hard up against a side panel there's going to be increased strain on the pins and vibrations are going to be focussed onto the pins, causing them to tilt in the shaft and you have the potential to create masses of heat if the contact area is significantly reduced. Side to side bends would do likewise as one side would be under tension, the opposite side would be under compression, again focussing the vibrations to joints 1 and 4 and leading to more arcing on those specific pins. This is possibly why it's pins 1 and 6 suffering eccessive heat buildup and melting before the inner pins.
It did not increased the heat because the plastuic cover which contained the heat was removed and now it became air-cooled. Wth an electrical tape it would increase the temperate faster but because of that the thermal camera would not read the temps, maybe only at the base...
I doubt that the melting happens because some wires disconnect it would rather because the resistens of the pins rise when they are not perfectly inside. The redistens depends on the area  of contact betwen plug and rifle and the lost energy by the resistens is heat  Addendum: The produced heat could cause the desolder of the cables, because there is no strain relief in the connector and accelerate the process further

If they're going to use a what's essentially a tiny bus bar to connect the wires together, the bar should have tabs that crimp onto the wires, then solder added in after crimping. That would ensure a solid connection that can stand up to some bending. With a solid crimp the solder would only serve to reduce electrical resistance by increasing the surface area that the wires are contacting, it would not be the primary means of mechanical attachment. Solder alone should not be used as the means of mechanical attachment on a connector that will see any sort of flexing or lateral force imo, it just isn't very strong. A bus bar style construction would be fine if they'd just do it right instead of half-assing the engineering.

Most industrial applications won't permit soldered joins like the as there is too high a stress concentration around the cable where the solder wicks into the braid. Nearly always use a crimped connector which crimps onto the insulation or has a collar to relieve stress. Obviously crimped connections can come loose over time but you are normally talking decades if the crimps have been designed and installed correctly. Probably done this to save themselves 0.5 cents per cable for a card costing as much as an ok laptop.

Failure of those plugs reminds me of melting of old tamiya battery plugs in my younger year. One thing I learned then was items heated up and melted where the greatest resistance was... the fact your photos showed it melting around the pins themselves makes me wonder if the individual pins and sockets are spec'd high enough for the amp draw. Breaking off a wire won't  increase the draw from a pin it would cause the feed lines to heat, essentially reducing the effective size of the wires feeding the plug.   What I do see from the pictures is potentially routing the wires pulling on and stressing the plug which is only retained by one center clip could cause slight loss of contact on the outer pins which essentially adds resistance and heat down near the plugs. This potentially aggravated by the thin walls of the plugs not able to clamp well enough to a pin that looks more suited to data than power delivery. It should be easy enough to test for with a simple resistance test with a multimeter while flexing the plug.  I am probably wrong, this is just my experience with plugs and power delivery on a hobby scale.

BTW - if the solder joint breaks within the plug, the consequences are much worse than just cutting the wire like you did.  These are DC voltages, and high Amp DC voltages.  The heat is caused by arching inside the connector whilst the cable is broken.  Take your cut cable, and constantly touch (make contact), and disconnect the cable again.  I'm fairly sure you'll see sparks, and your temps will also sky rocket.
It's time to stop playing with fragile multi pin connectors on high power cards and use something solid, like a bolt on lug with a substantially sized wire. Plus individual wires would be easier to route neatly. Maybe it's too basic, but simpler is almost always better. And it would for sure be cheaper to manufacture.
I think in your attempts to recreate the issue on your end was blundered due to the fact you cut the cables cleanly and disconnected them completely. In a real world scenario, the broken solder joint and pin would likely still be touching, or at most a micro gap, either of which would cause arcing, which in turn would cause the melting of the connector.   The melted outside connectors with broken joints isn't caused by the power draw of the other pins. If that were the case, the pins that are still properly connected would melt their own respective sections of the connector, not the surrounding sections.

Surely the worst case is if the solder joint cracks so there is some connection for the wire but with a high resistance generating heat. Actually you made a small error while testing. There is a difference between cutting the cable and braking connector pin. The difference is, that when connector is broken it is not working / not giving current to the via that pin. This means that the rest of the pins have to work harder and giving more heat. However in your test you have cut the cable, but the power supplied by other cables was still flowing through all the pins giving you a better result than you would have otherwise.

I see a problem in your test method trying to replicate this fault. You have completely removed the outer wire whereas under normal circumstances if it were to break the solder it would still be held in place still making contact with the rail, albeit a poor one. I work in the automotive industry working with 12v electronics and i see this melting problem fairly regularly with heater resistors rear light connectors and h4 headlamp connectors. The melting of these is always down to a poor but still present connection of the the terminal blade to pin(male to female part of the connector block). To identify this fault it needs to be done on a connector block that has suffered this problem, to see where the melting started.
@JayzTwoCents - A crack in the solder and what you're doing in this video are very different things. Like you said, Igor broke the tab off himself while trying to get the overmolded rubber off of the connector, that wasn't the source of the failure. A crack in the solder would result in power still being sent through that wire, yet through a smaller connection patch, meaning a higher resistance in that spot resulting in a higher amount of power being turned to heat which is then transferred to the pin where the plastic is the thinnest causing it to melt. Having the wire disconnected entirely as you do in this video would result in the power being sent through the remaining 3 wires only (200 watts each max) and wouldn't cause as much heat if any noticeable change at all. Through some light googling you will find that the 14 gauge wire used in this adaptor can handle around 192 watts at 12 volts so it would take a total draw of 576 watts to start building excess heat in the wire itself and there are very few situations where it would be drawing even that much power. This seems to be a bad design in the first place, but mostly bad quality control on the solder joint itself.

That is very odd. Molex don't even have a spec sheet for non-crimp terminals, and expecting solder joints to spread the load of 4 wires over 6 terminals is risky at best, but this does at least explain the symptoms seen.  I don't know what wire gauge this connector is using, but 18AWG wire is only rated for 10A @ 60¬∞C, so 4x would only be able to provide 480W before needing to get hotter than this.  I suspect they are using 16AWG wire (13A at 60¬∞C, so 624W, 4% headroom), but Molex don't do 16AWG crimp terminals, hence this solder/foil based 'hack'.  As it said in the video, if they had used individual 18AWG wires to each crimp terminal, this problem would have been pretty unlikely. Each wire would have had a lot more strain relief. Crimping is far safer than soldering when it comes to strain resistance. Even if they had soldered the outside wire to two terminals, and the inside wires to one terminal each, the extra strain relief might have made this sort of failure far less likely.  Ultimately though, this all boils down to the available headroom in the design.  The old Mini-fit connectors may have been conservative with their ratings (with 150W rating on a connector rated for over 400W), but with more than 50% headroom in the design, losing half of your connectors would not have caused a catastrophic failure, as can happens with this micro-fit based connector.

One cable to one pin is always preferable because you can make a type of strain relief out of a flared pad end. This entails using a piece of foil slightly wider than the wire and bending it into a U shape which reinforces the entire socket to wire. The single foil means there is not enough material between the wire connections to make a strain relieving bracket.

Just to mention something on the connector itself. In electronics we recommand 0.75mm¬≤ cross section to pass 7A, from igor's lab documents i found out that each pin of the 12VHPWR connector have 0.64mm width, that make 0.40mm¬≤ that should be used for 4A. By sending 600w it make 50A, so each pin has 8.33A to transmit which is way too much for the cross section they have. Soon as one of the connections fails it won't be the extra current on the rest that's creating the bulk of the heat. With the distances involved, 14ga wire should be able to handle running the full 50A (600W at 12V) off just two wires, and the three conductors on the 8-pin connector being at least 18ga should also be able to handle this even with just two of them connected - provided the two remaining connections weren't on a pigtail. And I would hope those upper-end PSUs are using 16ga wires instead. Not saying I'd want to have that much current through so few conductors of that size, but it shouldn't cause the kind of failures being seen.  But a broken contact which is still being held in place without being secured while being subjected to continual vibration (those fans might be smooth but they won't be perfect) is going to arc. Arcing generates a HUGE amount of heat, very quickly, which is why it's used for welding. This would very quickly melt the connector and damage the foil bus, potentially leading to a cascading failure unless you're "lucky" enough to have the arcing pieces separate quickly.

Jay, you relied on the wrong source for analysis of the problem.  See Buildzoid's analysis. Summary: Buildzoid suspects that the sockets are loosened enough through flexing to not contact the pins properly.  if the solder joints were bad, the heat would be generated at the joints at the top of the connector, but the failures show heat at the pin to socket interface, so it's not bad solder joints, but poor pin to socket contact that's causing the melting.

Jay, the case, that you're trying to reproduce, is not just dependent on +12V cable presence itself. When the solder breaks inside the plug (or these small metal traces leading from the metal strip to the connector breaks) the distance is relatively small, and with GPUs high current demand for solder joint or pin (>9A) it can cause micro sparking. Sparks equals heat and current fluctuations that lead to more sparks and especially heat generated on connector. Since the metal connector inside the plug has got the highest surface, it designates more heat to surrounding, hence melting plugs. Those splitters are under designed, and Nvidia should get back to pin-to-pin configuration.

4:55 - Number of cables connected to the plug shouldn't have any impact in the plug melting. Resistance of a cable is a physical value and it's constant. The only values that change are the voltage and current. The less cables are used with the same current value, the more they heat up. The bottleneck is always the thinnest point in the circuit, which is the plug. And yes - if there are poor solders in the plug they can be cracked creating even more bottleneck for the electric current.

The pins are melting 'at the end' of the connector and not at the junction between connector and wires. Thermal camera actually reads the temp of the plastic that is lower than the temp at the pins junction and the test bench is not a real case scenario. This is an issue that probably starts with a crack inside the encased junction between wires and connector (which cause higher resistance and rising temperature) and degenerate in a cascade failure (electrical arc at the junction between cable pin and card pin) due to poor contact caused by thermal expansion of the pin connector-side.

The melting happened on the outside pins which indicates the inside pins are not overloaded.  It the inside pins were overloaded they should be the ones that are melting.  It sounds like the outside pins are still connected but the joint has a higher impedence than normal.  Meters that can measure low impedences are expensive but there are some low cost devices made for vaping that can measure resistance down to a small fraction of an ohm.  . At high currents a tiny increase in impedence of the connection can cause a significant rise in operating temperature.

Your explanation doesn‚Äôt make much sense to me. If you‚Äôre claiming that the outer conductors are breaking so that all the current is flowing through the inner two conductors in the plug then naturally it should be the inner two conductors in the plug that get hottest and that‚Äôs where you should see the melting not on the outer two conductors which you are theorizing are not carrying any current. I think what‚Äôs more likely to be happening is that the solder joints on the outer ends of the plugs are partially breaking meaning that the current which would normally go through a nice Wide solder joint is now going through part of a solder joint or a point contact so the current density at the solder joint of the outer conductors is much higher because you have the same amount of current flowing through a smaller cross section.  If the broken solder joint is still making contact that makes the resistance of the contact go up markedly and therefore so should the heat Which is why you see the outer plugs melts and not the inner ones.

I suspect that the issue is not in having cables totally disconnected from the pins, but when there is a crack in the solder the resistance on that pin will go up causing the heating problem.  If the cable is disconnected completely from the pin the power is distributed across the the other wires.  They and the plug are likely to get hotter but it is when power is still passing through a high resistance joint, poor or partially broken solder that the resistance, and hence the heat really goes up then the pin that has suffered the damage can cause melting around it.  You were actually creating a different fault in your test here.

When I researched solder vs crimp for power cables last year the one take-away I got was that if you solder the cables, some amount of solder will be sucked into the cable itself and it will harden it, making it easier to break because you essentially created a kind of lever. Thats why crimping cables is the superior technique because the cable stays flexible.

Hey Jay, The reason of the outer lines getting smoked is the fragile and weakened connection, not - as you tried to recreate - the higher load of the middle lines (if true, there should be the melding going on). The true reason is the weak connection of the outer lines, creating a bottleneck (resistance) for the current, which results in big heat a this exact spots. If you cut it, there will be no heatspot at all, but the other lines will compensate the current by gaining a few degrees more....  High resistance = high heat

Also of note, 2 plugs from the power supply end go to 4 pins on the graphics card, then 2 plugs from the power supply go to 8 pins. This means the outside pins are having to draw higher wattage thru them, thus resulting in higher temps/wear on the 2 outside pins on each side. (pause at 4:59 and look closely at the photo). If each cable draws 150 watts, the outside will draw 75 watts per pin, the inside 4 wires will draw 37.5 watts per pin. Any good electronic or electrical engineer know that you should never ever solder stranded wire to a surface (worst) or a hole (bad) in a rigid plate/PCB and only solder a wire to  another wire. The reason is that tin based solder melts at 220-260¬∞C and is brittle below the meeting point, so any mechanical stress creates bad contacts over time. If it was soldered with copper based solder, that's something else, copper is ductile so it rarely breaks, but it's also much much more difficult to melt. Anyway soldered wires should only be for hobbyists and any professional should only use crimping or wire wrapping! A hobbyists or non-electrically educated person would think that stranded wire is more flexible and relaxes the stress for to any wire movement but it's deeply wrong : when stranded wire is soldered, copper being highly conductive, the solder melting temperature doesn't stay on the contact zone but travels up the wire and so does the liquid solder due to capillary effect!  So you end up with a wire full of solder between the strand a few centimeters up the solder joint, creating a strong lever out of the flexible strands, quickly breaking the solder joint...

It's not about the remaining cables carrying more load per, it's the gapped connection itself. You're correct that that gap increases resistance, which increases heat. Welcome to the primary danger of aluminum household wiring.
The 12VHPWR connector itself might not be the cause of the issue, but it's demonstrably less safe than the alternative (3 or 4 8-pin connectors). The melting issue being so prevalent is likely a combination of user error (which is to be expected, people are only human) and bad manufacturing (NVidia's adapter). But, again, it's only causing such drastic issues because it's a bad connector design.

5:50 Again for the countless time... i forgot the count. Jay has very small knowledge about electricity, electronics and cables.  When a solder point is about to break off, and the connection is under a load. This will create a lot of heat very fast!  Simple way of explaining it is, when the electricity has to transfer it self from the wire to the connector. And the solder point has cracked and is falling apart, this will create a lot of electrical turbulence in the flow. Which will cause heat to build up in the cracked part of the solder, as we know plastic will melt way before metals here used. The plastic housing will start to slowly melt until it will start a thermal run away point where the whole connector heats up super fast and starts melting the plastic around the connection transfer points. The speed of this all depends how high the loads are on the GPU, you could use the GPU for weeks on low load (100W-200W) and only that first time when you start going 300W,400W +500W it will start accelerating that thermal run away point.  And i think this is the problem with the lab lasting, cause they always have tested with perfect contacts and not broken solder points. The board from 3090ti have load balance with diferent parts of the conector and it was saving the conectors from burning, this load balance is not present in the 4090. The power draw will folow the path of less resistence. Ex: If 3 out of 6 of those conectors are getting any more resistence than normal, the power will flow through the 2 ones with less resistence and exceed the specifications (creating the problem), with power balance the board will not let it happen. Probably the split casing of the conector with bending is creating this bad conection with some of the conectors and causing this unbalance. The 12vhpwr have ~14% of overspec current, and with 12conectors 6 each polarity theoretically you can have only 1 of the pins out or with a bad conection and stay with in spec.

If you break that terminal strip or the solder joint, the housing is not going to allow it to completely separate, leaving you with a loose joint - the most common cause of electrical fire. Basically, the original joint is reduced in cross-sectional area, thereby reducing conductivity at the joint, increasing resistance, and creating heat when current flows.

From Igor's teardown it seems pretty clear that the most likely culplret here is either poorly and inconsistently soldered connections causing high internal resistance and then heat as a byproduct, and/or movement of the cable causing the solder joints to weaken or come away from the pads partially, which would cause even higher internal resistance and more heat. It's unlikely the cable would come away from the pad entirely while everything is being held together.  Having the wire come away entirely would actually be the better scenario as a severed connection would not generate any heat, but would cause the still functioning pins to be loaded beyond their capacity provided the sense wire is still telling the card that the full 600W is available as Jay describes here.   I'm betting the outcome of this is that in all cases either poorly soldered or broken internal connections are to blame.

Jay you are doing it wrong! The outer pins melt because of poor contact between the pad and the wire. There is still a tiny little bit of contact and that is the place of high resistance thus temperature. Don't cut the pads off you are rerouting the current to the center pins. Important: As you said, "Pushing and pulling can break the solder joint". That's the force when bending the cables sideways against each other.
Part of the problem is likely when the card is under high load, and you're literally at the moment where the cables/foil starts to part... essentially you're creating potential for an arcing scenario; which can generate very high temperatures; which would drastically exacerbate the issues.
If a cable is physically damaged (e.g. by walking on an extension cord or excessively stretching it), the insulation resistance decreases, current can flow through the damaged section from one conductor to another, after which the insulation will start to carbonize and let even more current pass. This is a positive feedback situation‚Äîmore current causes more carbonization, which causes more current‚Äîthat eventually leads to the cable burning and possibly starting a fire. New houses require AFCI breakers on outlets to detect such faults. Perhaps the bending stresses of the 4090 cables is causing the insulation to break down, rather than the solder joints.
Could be wrong, but I think you guys are chasing a red haring here. Doing the math of my side, if I assume a 50A draw, and a 20 mOhm resistance down one path, and a 1 Ohm resistance down another, then the parallel resistance is ~19.6 mOhm by the work equation. The voltage drop due to the parallel path is then ~1v by Ohms Law V=IR. If I investigate eath path separately knowing that the voltage drop across each will be the same, then using V=IR, the 20 mOhm path carries 49A whereas the 1 Ohm path carries only 1A. Then @ the 1 volt voltage drop using W=IV, that's only 1W heat for the 1 ohm path, but 49W heat for the other. i.e. provided that there exists another low resistance path to carry the current, then a high resistance path (with constant current) equates to less heat, not more. There's a simple way to test this by doing a thought experiment. Take a high current load with the proper sized (thick) wire feeding it power, then take a thin 30 gauge wire and put it in parallel with the proper sized wire. Do you expect the 30 gauge wire to melt? No. Its relative high resistance will protect it because the properly sized wire will carry the vast majority of the current.

You cut off the wire, but the bus connecting the other pins is still in tact... so there isn't going to be a temperature rise since the load is still distributed across all 6 pins.
The word you're looking for is "bus"... a metal strip used as a common connector for more tan one electronic trace.  A "busbar" is larger and used for higher voltage electrical wiring, often with terminals.

Jay, I know you said everyone has an opinion (and as an electrician) I think I can explain the issue. And you are partly correct. It is an issue with the wire breaking off the metal pad. But because it is still held in place by the plastic casing the wire is arcing causing the heat build up and why the end contacts are melting and the more this happens with the outer end connections, the more the heat will travel to the middle connectors and the heat could melt the center connections and then they start to arc, then you are in trouble 10x worse. This is common in electrical panels that someone hasn't tighten down a screw and the arcing happens and you see melting of the wire. If the wire is 14 awg @ 12v rated 192watts @ 24v rated 384watts (charts online). So cutting the wire completely won't recreate the issue.

Maybe you need light contact at the broken solder. Light contact can create high resistance while allowing current to pass, greatly increasing heat at the high resistance open. acting like the wires in a toaster, which are just high resistance wires that allow current to pass through creating heat.   A broken connection held in place by the plastic housing can have the same high resistance effect. Cutting the wire completely wouldn't necessarily create enough resistance in the remaining wires to create a high heat condition. It may pass more current but the resistance in the wire may still be low enough to prevent overheating.

Also seeing a lot of people who think the poor connection leads to extra draw on the good connections, however the thats not quite accurate. Poor connection causes resistance, resistance causes power to dissipate as heat. That‚Äôs why you can run 100A through a larger wire without issue, but if you try running 100A through a small wire (ex. 20 gauge) it will burn up.
Your plug had more actual solder on the pads and seemed built correctly (manufacturing process). Those pads/pin bases are crazy thin for that kind of amperage load. There are many videos out there about why engineers leave solder mask (color covering) off the traces on circuit boards in high amperage areas so solder can be deposited. Thicker traces copper and solder carry higher loads. This is why yours was able to withstand more current over a long period of time. Your ultimate conclusion is correct.

Hi Jay, from my experience you actually will not see abnormally high temperatures by cleanly cutting one of the cables. I believe the issue is when the solder joint is cracked, or completely broken but still held in place by the housing. This will introduce a much higher resistance zone and probably lots of arcing. So allowing the current to still flow through the "broken" connection, but much less efficiently will cause much higher resistance and thus heat. I don't claim to be an expert but, I do work as a computer engineer in industrial automation and see this type of failure pretty frequently.
I work for Molex (the maker of that connector) albeit I'm not part of the connector division but I have seen many failures of electronics in general so I'll put my two cents in.  I am not sure what the pin outs are for atx12 volt but the connector is rated for 5 amps for each pin plus a margin of safety so I do not think these connectors are melting because one pin breaks and others are taking more load. What I think is going on here is that one connector is cracking but not fully broken (from repeated insertion and/or bending) cause an increase in resistance (think of a lot of current through a small wire) and cause it too heat up and melt. Furthermore, if the connection is broken, there may be arcing which again causes heat and failure of the connector.

@JayzTwoCents - I had a thought. I don't 100% know how applicable it is, which I'll get to, but it was a thought. And this is not me saying, "Oh I know what the problem is." This is me saying, "I have a hypothesis but literally zero means to test it."  I used to work for a third-party high-voltage electrical insulation systems testing laboratory. (Yeah, it's a mouthful.) We did the third-party testing that ALL of the US, Japan, China, Germany, etc. manufacturers of transformers, motors, and generators, rely upon to get their certifications from certifying bodies (UL, CE, TUV, etc.) 99.9% of what we dealt with was AC, not DC, so I genuinely don't know how well it would apply, but I have a suspicion that it might apply anyway.  I wonder if the problem is less about making three 8-pin connectors do the work of four, and more about arcing inside the connector when a solder joint breaks.  See, with AC, it takes 30-32 kV to cause an arc across a 1-inch air gap. (We had a cool demonstration apparatus to illustrate this, and I no longer have the video of it, but it is what it is.) We're obviously talking about much, MUCH smaller gaps than 1-inch here, and admittedly DC *does* behave differently than AC.  However, the thought that came to mind from watching this and other videos on the issue was this - what if the solder joint broke, but remained physically close enough to the bridging foil to allow the voltage to arc across the gap? An electrical arc, AC or DC, generates WAY more heat than a somewhat-overloaded-but-still-solidly-connected solder joint. If the solder joint broke, but was close enough to arc, it would suuuuper easily melt the plastic (likely a Delrin derivative) that makes up the connector.
That foil thing is called a buss wire/bar

I wanted to note what your saying about the cause of the heat issues is dead on. Here is a little evaluation from an engineering perspective verifying what you said.  Just some quick math. A #14 awg wire is designed to handle 15amps. Amps= Watts/Volts. Current will evenly distribute, so 600Watts will distribute 12.5Amps to each of the 4 wires. If you loose one wire leaving you with 3 wires, the amps per wire will increase to 16.667 Amps pushing1.667 Amps above the rated amperage of the #14 Wire causing it to over heat.   To avoid potential injury on existing connectors I would recommend a strain relief at the exit of the connector.
Please bear in mind that your FLIR can't accurately read the temperature of bare metal, since its emissivity is very low. You'll get good readings off the nearby plastic, but the traditional thermographers' hack is to stick a bit of matte black tape (I like Taeyoung electrical tape) onto a metal surface and then measure the temperature of the tape. A spritz of black spraypaint also works well.

This 12-pin connector is such a train wreck. What a horrible design.
high power wire has been known as a failure point when soldered for a long time, properly done, solder is ok, over a crimp.  tin at 0c is at a temperature that will allow creep, it's an odd metal that way, any tension will loosen it after a while.
In short, you only describer one of the possible corner cases, the connector cable combo is IMHO mechanically unfit for the purpose.  hate to say it, but the connector is made of plastic which might be less stiff than the cable and the pins. At full load, if the male pin is at an angle to the female contact and it will touch it at a much smaller surface, it will result in higher resistance ergo will be prone to carbonisation only exacerbating the resistance leading to melting due to high temp. With hundreds of amps you will always risk a problem of overheating, the connector is just not mechanically sturdy enough for the challenge.  People would say that at NVIDIA the engineers would have known this, though I ve seen the same mistake made at top aerospace companies and I d say for satellites they have comparatively better electrical engineers than NVIDIA and still make a bad judgement call sometimes. Fundamentally, the fix is to desolder the original onboard connector and to replace it with possibly a metallic body microd power connector with a dclick (google it) or better mechanically sound solution.  It does not change the fact that the harness is nominally fit for purpose and the calculation nominally fits, though the mechanical properties combined with the use case render the solution unreliable and dangerous, likely with a higher quality harness too i.e. I would be hesitant to recommend alternate cables

I think my greatest concern is that the plug is soldered with no mechanical retention.  Sure, the current may not get hot enough to MELT the solder, but solder will get ductile as it heats up.  Put any mechanical load on a soldered joint and run enough current through it, and it will eventually work it's way loose in my opinion.   Soldered connections are fine on a PCB where there are no mechanical loads, but WHY would they use a soldered connection on a high current line under mechanical strain? It makes no sense to me.
I'd imagine being in a case would make a huge difference. My asus 3080 in my fractal meshify makes the glass so hot next to the card I can almost not touch the glass, with gpu temp at 80c. You need to test the 40 series in that manner. Glass conducts the heat so well and with the cable right up on it, I bet you get more than a few degrees more in temperature.

Jay, worst than no contacct at all is a bad contact (aka high resistance, but not open). If they cold solder barly touch the foil, then a hot spot is created. It's like having a tiny tiny wire with a lot of current going to it. In your case, there are other ways for the electricity to travel and this shouldn't allow for over heating on the bad contact.   On the other hand, if all the wires from a single phase (rail) are cut due to desoldering or breaking pins, then there might be heat created through the bad contact from that rail trying to push its current though a small wire.   For exemple, having 3 wires from the first rail on the 3 first solders spots and the last solder spot is being fed from another rail. If this last solder fails and make bad contact, then the heat would spread through pin and then the plastic and melt the connector.  This is my theory... Not sure if I'm clear. It is used to simulate that the flammable liquids used in electrical and electronic equipment may ignite due to overheating and leak out of the fire protection enclosure, causing electrical fires and endangering the reliable operation of the equipment and personal safety.  Test principle:  Fill 10mL of flammable liquid with the specified performance (average calorific value 38MJ/L, mass per unit volume between: 0.845g/mLÔΩû0.865g/mL, flash point: 43.5‚ÑÉ~93.5‚ÑÉ) in the metal ladle, after heating to the distillate fuel oil‚Äôs ignition point and burning 1mim, the motor drives the spoon to rotate at a certain height (100mm) and a certain flow rate (1mL/s), and then steadily pour the flammable liquid on the center of the opening of the sample (fire protection enclosure). Why Jay's test failed to create a failure: 1) The rubber acts as an insulator, allowing the wires to get hotter 2) Power will still flow over the "crack" in the solder, but at a much higher resistance. It may even arc. Hey, I was right! lol I had said that's it's most likely down to manufacturing defects. In all seriousness though, the reason why Nvidia made the cable like this is because of MSI. MSI's Gamer Trio series uses the same 16 pin connector but only has 3, 8-pin PCIE connectors on the other end. This means Nvidia had to make their connector solution modular to allow all possible configuration types with the same connector/adaptor and allow the AIBs to customize the adaptor cable. Hence, the bridged connection. Looking at the design, we can probably say that some form of strain relief or using a thicker plate with mechanical hardware (screws) backed up by soldering would have given a much larger margin of error with the adaptor cables. Nvidia had these cables designed to the extreme end to cut costs leaving a tiny amount of human error variability left. Their supplier couldn't meet the human error variability requirements nor could they have their inspectors able to catch failed units as a proper control.
If it moves, it has to be crimped, very simple rule of thumb, and then the crimp is supposed to be secured in a housing and not exposed. Though generally any wiring should have strain relief if wiring is accessible  Also the broken off wires will spark or have very high resistance, which will create way more heat than the remaining 4
@Jay - Your final commentary is correct.  Heat is not generated by a broken / open circuit.  It is generated by high current passing through either highly resistive material, or or in this case INADEQUATE material, to carry the load.  So, rather than CUTTING the wire entirely, if you had cut or removed say 50-75% of the copper strands at the solder joint, but kept the circuit intact, I bet you'd have seen some melting and/or fire.
A few observations. That's "foil" is properly called a "bus bar". Bus bars are usually much better built than that. They usually also have strain reliefs to ensure they're not subjected to physical force. Gamers Nexus was blabbing on about how the 4090 adjusted it's load across the 8-pin leads to equalize current draw - that's bupkis. The four leads are connected to individual +12v and GND busbars. Nvidia can use the sense pins to determine how much total to draw, but that's the grand total of the adjustment.
Gaps in wires create arcing. Arcing creates heat. That's where wire insulation melts and can cause fire.
I can't find one detail that is quite important here. Are the pins bridged also on the GPU itself or is the bridge in the adapter the only one? If they are bridged on the GPU, then the flimsy bridge in the adapter should not cause heating up. Electricity will find the path of least resistance and would simply go through the middle 4 pins, then spread to all 6 lanes. If it's the only bridge, then it might be possible that the card tries to pull more power from the outside pins and the only path for it to go is through the tiny bridge. The problem here is that nobody reproduced this behavior, slight increase in temperatures might be just a result of using 5 or 4 out of 6 cables and joints, not enough to cause a meltdown. Besides that, if that was the point that generates too much heat, why would only the outside pins melt? It's a bridge between outside pins and the second from the outside. It would actually make more sense that the outside pins have better cooling than the ones inside and it would melt from inside out. In all cases it's the outside pin that gets hot enough to melt the plastic, so the heat source should not really be in between two pins and only cause one to melt. Even if we'd consider that the outside pin has lower thermal mass (cable ripped off) than the second from outside (cable still connected and bridged to the 3rd one), it would look quite unusual. What seems to be much more likely cause is that the cable is only slightly ripped off. Enough to still let the current through but with much higher resistance causing  much more heat. This way the heat source would be only on the outside pin and the second from the outside would get less of this heat transferred through the bridge. This would also match everything we know so far. It's caused by bending it and cracking the solder or the whole pad, the cable is still connected but the connection is very small and heats up. In this case it would not matter if it's bridged or not but rather if the cable can partially rip off the pin pad. The way to test it would be to cut only a bit of the pad of the outside pins and see if they heat up under load. Probably we would see some spectacular meltdown. Even more, it might be reproducible only on setups when the outside pins are connected to separate rails in the power supply. After all, if it was just one rail split into two wires and one of those wires has higher resistance because of the cut, most of the current would divert through the one that is in tact. If they are separate rails (not bridged anywhere else between PSU and the adapter), it is possible that it would try to pull fairly high current through the damaged pad as there is no other path for it to go and it is still connected. Instead of this sort of testing, I would just go on with manual test by putting a load on each pin lines separately and testing how it behaves, what's the temperature and how bending it affects the results. Most likely under high load on the outside pin and the outside wire you will see resistance spike when you bend it enough.  What's important is that it's not enough to have this bottleneck that will heat up under high current but also have a situation in which the current would actually flow there. If there is a bridge on GPU side, it will divert to use pins with less resistance. If there is a bridge on PSU side, it will divert to use pin pads with less resistance. Only if there are no bridges on both sides and the bridge in the adapter has fairly high resistance, it might actually pull the power straight through the broken pad that has much higher resistance. Simply because there's no better way for it to flow.
