I think you're on the right track here. The flexing can also cause fractures in a bad solder joint that don't fully break the joint or the metal pad. This defect will increase the resistance of the joint and cause heating at greater loads. That would cause those outer pins to start to heat up like people are seeing. Trying to solder wires right at a flexing joint like that is always going to be weak. The old style where you have an individual wire crimped into each pin will always be more robust.

I've ordered 10 replacement connectors... wonder if I'll ever have someone needing these replaced :D  For those that have any worry about a bad connection, if you have a multimeter, do a continuity test on one end to the other.  If you have continuity testing fine on all cables except one, bend if and see if the connection comes back.  Easiest way to tell if a cable is good or not.  And quicker than ripping a cable apart.

This makes almost perfect sense. But if all the power is going through the middle two and that was the heat source that would be where the burn is. I almost guarantee the broken solder joints are still touching, but only slightly. Ever see a loose connector on a car battery? It can melt the terminal straight into the battery.  The worse the connection the more resistance there is and the more heat is generated.
If the solder joint breaks but the wire is still "making contact" with the metal foil connector, that is where the heat will come from. The bad joint is still carrying current but the high resistance and possible arcing of the failed solder joint will create a lot of heat. If the joint breaks and the wire doesn't stay in contact with the metal foil connector, the heat increase will be in the three remaining wires, but will be much less substantial than current flowing through a bad solder connection.

I feel like the issue isn't that the connection is broken, it's that it's broken but NOT far enough removed so the electricity is arcing from the wire to the pad which would cause more heat.
If the wires of the connector are forced to be canted in either side, the solder _may_ melt with the connector being improperly used (even if it is firmly seated ‚Äî a seat is not a guarantee) and due to this, form  a worse connection with the card out of sight from the average end-user. Note to self: if going vertical, and using nVidia's stupid cost-optimized adapter, zip-tie to something parallel the cable for preventing the canting which leads to this defect.  Or, just go with CableMod's solutions. That 90-degree 12VHPWR adapter looks _very_ nice and pairing that with one of their cables would be even better.
I think the term is busbar; a very small one, but all the wires connected to it then the pins too, it allows the power to equalise across all contacts. Cracking it isolates a pin to a wire, forcing all the power of that wire thru Only that pin. It's interesting; I think the busbar can equalise for load & heat W basic physics; any pin the gets hot also increases its resistance relative to any other, which lowers it's capacity & raises the load on the others. That lowered load allows the hot pin to cool, and so on; probably happening at tiny fractions of a second, IE hundreds of thousands of times a second.This means (unless the bus breaks) that load & heat automatically self level. Of course, as soon as the bar cracks, you're in trouble. Theoretically, a reinforced busbar should solve the issue. I wouldn't be surprised if they just sauter a conductive wire across there to provide that fix until/unless they roll out a solution at the plug factory. The other fix is to sauter the wires to the center of the available pafes, avoiding the outer pads, or bridging them to inner ones. This would avoid the pads at risk of breaking or provide alternate pathways if a break happens. At the end of the day, I'm pretty sure the plugs are designed to break there to crimp to & isolate the designed six wires (per side) from each other; this failure reflects the supplier misuse of the connector.

Also any power connection that is flexible usually should be crimped only and not soldered because stranded wire can be flexed multiple times without breaking but once you solder it then it tends to break under stress since it is now effectively a solid wire and if you bend a solid wire several times it will break

The heat isn't being generated by the additional power going through the undamaged center pins -- if it was they would be the ones melting. The heat is being generated by power still going through the damaged pins and the damage causing increased resistance, increased resistance causes increased heat.
The difference here is you removed the pin entirely from the connector. If it had cracked/broke in the housing then it would not only increase the resistance but also bridge or arc from the vibrations.

Extra current going through the center wires would not melt the outer ones, that makes no sense. But, a thinned connection on the outer ones would create higher resistance and turn the metal into a filament, like an electric lighter or light bulb. It's more likely that the flexing caused a weak connection, not a broken one.
So with that being said, and considering there's 3 +12V and 3 GND per 8 pin connector, a potential solution could be to splice 2 into 1 on all 12 of each connection, leaving you with 12 total wires. But I do understand why they added the bus bar in the connector, it was probably to attempt to equally distribute the load over all 4 of the 8 pin connectors.

The "foil" interconnect point cracking can reduce current transfer from pin to pin internally of the connector but it is likely not the problem.  The melting damage in most user cases seen is at the "pin and barrel" which is not a point affected by the "foil" interconnect.  Inserting the connector at an angle is likely the largest culprit of causing the barrel side of the pin/barrel connection point to stress apart thus reducing the physical contact point and force.  Even some foreign debris when installing the connector could cause this issue.  It is quite common on industrial connectors...

It might not be as simple as just disconnecting the one cable or breaking the one solder joint.  It could be that as the solder joint is in the process of making a full break it cracks, but doesn't fully break.  That will cause the resistance to climb and heat to rise.  At some point it does eventually break completely.  As it breaks it will cause some arcing. So it COULD be that once that break is made that there is continuous arcing inside the connector, which creates a lot of excess heat.  If you wanted to test that, you'd have to measure+trace the current on each individual wire and see if there are any current spikes on wire connected to the failing solder joint.

Someone correct me if im wrong, but a fully disconnected wire would not increase temp in the connector. The cables to the plug would get more heat due to the added current they would need to carry. Which is the main concern about running 2 pig tail connectors to the card.  What would increase temp in the connector would be a partial connection with tons of resistance dumping heat into the metal foil and then the pins. If the added heat from the wires were to be the issue causing temp increase in the connector, all the pins would get the same heat. (With a gradient depending on the thermal conductivity of the material)  Edit: Someone else mentioned arcing, but i dont think that is as big of a concern considering low voltage and somewhat low current (~12A). It could happen, but then there would most likely be a fire unless the psu trips an OC protector

I think you're on to something, but your explanation does not actually logically make sense.  If the outer solder connections are actually broken, and only the inner ones are conducting power, then the point of heat generation should be the _inside_ conductors, not the ones on the end, so the inside pins should melt down first.  The outer pins are not carrying any current anymore, so are also not generating any heat.  What I suspect may be more likely is that movement back and forth ends up breaking some of the individual strands of the stranded wire where they are soldered to the contacts (or possibly they weren't even fully soldered to begin with), so that they are still technically connected, but only partially, making for higher resistance.  The card is still pulling power through them at the same rate, but because they are damaged (but not completely disconnected) they generate more heat under that load than they should.  Another possibility is that the wire stays fully connected to the pad, but the pad breaks away from the rest of the "bus bar" that the others are connected to, so one of the four wires is only going to one pin of the connector, and cannot share its heat/load with the rest of them.  That would presumably end up pulling more current over a single pin than it is supposed to take (generating more heat) and also reduce the ability for it to dissipate that heat at the same time.

This is 100% answer. No, really. I tested these connectors in my lab during the week with Industrial reference power supply. My conclusion is: solder joints solution is really poorly consisted I understand they used it this way too save about 3-5 mm of space on chunky cards what they should have done is not solder wires directly to terminal as wire. Using locking faston and then soldering it that way would eliminate all issues. I have not seen on any of cables use of flux. This is also huge mistake and saving 2-5 pennies on plug.   Technical:  Using microscope I was able to verify issue of solder forming micro fractures. Under such a high load this creates (technical term) TRANSITION RESISTANCE. Where as normal solder joint would create .01-.5  ohm resistance I was able to measure up to 10 ohms on the worst one. This will cause joint behaving as heating element. This can happen also on consumer goods. Let‚Äôs in theory say you have Chinese knock off USB C  that does not click into socket properly and you are using 160W to charge your laptop and have it contact well enough to start charging but the pins got lots of tiny air gaps in contact points. Honestly I know this is consumer market but at this power level it‚Äôs just better to use spring clip terminal or screw terminal. There is no way this should pass EU CE. And it would definitely not pass any industrial safety standards for fire safety. Dude is not the bending, it's just that the 2 center 8 pin connector spread load on 2 pins for ground and 2 for power and the outside ones only 1 pin  ground  and 1 power, for the same amperage, nothing to do with bent copper unless is yanking the connector out partially.
Looking at Igor's pictures, I think you hit the nail on the head about the bad solder job.  That should have been caught if they did even just a basic visual inspection. The factory probably skipped the step because 1, QC is expensive and time consuming, and 2, Most common soldering issues can't be QC'd.  So what could NVidia's factory have done differently?  1.  Engineering: When soldering a wire to a device, it needs to have 3 points of contact.  That's the bare minimum of the cheapest industry accepted standard. There are 2 common ways to achieve this: First, they could have specified the wire be bent in a J around a pole on the connector (which isn't there - more on that in the next sentence), or second, they could have put the wire through a hole and ensured both sides had a fillet. The foil bridge looks like a custom part for this cable design, and a stamped custom part at that.  They could have easily added a pole or a hole without even increasing the cost, just by designing their stamp to include the feature.  Either way, they would have an inherently much more reliable connection and the soft plastic overmold would be a bit wider but oh freaking well, there's no mechanical interference in that spot. Exploring one of these methods would also likely allow them to dip solder or wave solder rather than hand solder, as the foil is less likely to fall off the wire while moving the partial assembly.  2. Engineering: Don't specify solder joints when you can crimp or ultrasonic weld the part. both processes are way more reliable and consistent, but more importantly they can be quality-controlled cheaply whether you use manual or automated methods. Crimping or ultrasonic welding both also use less material AND less labor.  3. Manufacturing: Even though soldering can't be QC'd for most issues, they could at least freaking look at the part. The reason the most common soldering issues can't be QC'd is because the obvious ones are easily caught and corrected.  4. Manufacturing: That employee who made Igor's connector is clearly phoning it in. Get rid of them or retrain. If someone solders all day there's no reason any of their work should ever look like Igor's part.  5. Vendor: Are we sure these are genuine parts?   So in conclusion - Looks like NVidia outsourced the manufacture of these parts and went with someone who cut costs in the wrong places. Rather than investing in tooling and automation, they cut costs by using untrained or underpaid/unmotivated labor and outdated methods.   As an aside, your connector is soldered in a way that looks like it was done properly.  I don't think the thickness of the plate is necessarily a problem - that part is the same between your and Igor's harnesses and its thickness is controlled by the raw material vendor (And raw material vendors don't generally screw that up without being forced to correct the issue real quick). Instead, I think the crappy soldering job on Igor's cable likely has a higher-than-expected resistance connection across all the wires.  So all are already out of spec but then one breaks off on top of that. This would explain why you can't necessarily replicate the issue on a good part.   If you did want to recreate the issue, you could probably do it by de-soldering, then doing everything possible wrong while re-soldering. Cold soldering iron, not pre-wetting the wire, not enough flux, wrong timing, and so on.

Great video as usual.      Not to join the bandwagon, but the conclusion that its the fault of the bridge carrying current to the last pin when the connector breaks doesn't sit right with me for one reason - there are multiple examples where only the outermost pin is completely melted. If the foil bridge to the last pin is too small causing it to heat up to the point of melting the plastic all the way at the end of the pin, since its the bridge itself heating up, it should also be heating the other pin the bridge connects to just as much.       That is of course only a valid conclusion IF the load is balanced evenly over the pins. If the outside pin is being asked for twice as much current as its neighbor, then the pin itself could be contributing to its overheating in addition to any extra heat from the bridge due to a broken solder joint. I'm not saying the bridge isn't heating up, I'm saying I think there is another factor exacerbating the issue to the point of melting. I have absolutely zero proof of it, but if I HAD to toss my hat into the ring, I'd say that improper load balancing had something to do with it. For all I know, the pins all get combined back into one connection immediately after the connector so there isn't any load balancing at all. Like I said, I have no clue.      And no, I don't think its arcing causing the issue. Evidence of arcing severe enough to cause that much heat would be plainly visible, especially if it was arcing to the thin foil bus bar. Plus arcing would be happening much higher up in the connecter than where the damage is seen occurring.    (Obligatory "iM aN eNgInEeR" post. A mechanical engineer, but I think I took enough circuits, physics, and materials science classes that I'm allowed to weigh in)  Edit before any replies come in: I'll happily eat my words if I'm wrong and it ends up being entirely the fault of the bridge, but I doubt it.

Honestly, I think Igor just got this plain wrong. In fact Jay's testing pretty much demonstrates that. If the (badly designed, badly made - nasty) soldered cable connections were the issue then the overheating would be at the back of the connector near the soldered joints. Instead the problem is very clearly at the front where the pins and sockets meet. Remember the PCI-SIG "thermal deviance" report, which showed single pins delivering well over 300 W and hitting 180degC? They were NOT using the nasty Nvidia 4x8pin adaptor. (When you mingle multiple parallel paths for a current to flow, the high-resistance paths stay cold and it's the low-resistance paths that overheat; kinda unintuitive but that's how it works and the PCI-SIG report demonstrates that beautifully.)

7:55 I think it's a bad idea, possibly even a bit irresponsible, to discount the connection sockets themselves. That _still_ doesn't discount the issue of wear relating to the 30 insertion cycle rating, or the effect lateral forces has on deformation of those sockets. You can _also_ get a similarly bad connection as the socket warps and loosens over time, and lateral forces on the pins could make that worse. I think it's a bad idea to find _one singular lone sole cause,_ and then simply stop with any further testing for other potential fault scenarios, as I think there are multiple failure modes possible with these.  I can't exactly post the image here, due to limitation of youtube comments, but I worked for a manufacturer of electric motors, and we used a similar connector for low voltage signaling. Even with lower voltages, the number one reason for RMAs was the connector failing intermittently. I posted an image online of one of these connectors next to an 8-pinpower connector. You can see how square the thicker metal of the8-pin connector is, despite multiple insertion cycles, but the smaller connector's sockets are _visibly warped_ out of a square shape, and are open on one end. This is also an unfortunately ideal scenario for a high resistance heat generating contact. You should not discount the pin socket connections.

IMHO, the problem is BAD connection between "side" pin and its closest 12V lead - BAD connection (not completely severed connection) increases impedance hence more loses hence more heat hence melting. If side pin's connection to leads is broken, there will be no current flowing through it - no heat. Situation here is reversed - SIDE pin starts melting - but this does NOT HAVE TO mean more current - it can just be more impedance (heating).  If reverse would be true, lead (12V) cables would start melting, not pin's housing.  EDIT: This is actually what Igor says - broken connection to side pins, with intermittent connection - end result - heat

Jay! It's when there's an unclean break that resistance increases. When there's still little contact left. The break happends inside of that glued wrapping, you're testing it completely detached outside of its wrap. Inside the wrap it would still contact but with  less contact area. It has nowhere to go if it breaks in its wrap. So it's probably micro-contacting with the vibration from fans. Shifting contact areas along the break. Meaning also shifting load on the incoming strand. Regardless much more resistance with being semi-attached. Becoming a toaster-wire üòá
The very fact they didn't think about 'voltage increase detection' makes me think they weren't thinking about Ohm's Law.  If you have a graphics card pulling the same wattage, but if the resistance of the power connector has increased due to poor connection, it then needs to  then increase the voltage to push the same wattage. Therefore, the voltage drop (with a higher delivery voltage), given the same current, is also much higher, and the heat dissipation is a lot higher as a result.

OK lets look at it this way 450WATT at 12V = 37.5AMP = 16 AWG Wire can carry 13.5 AMP safely which means 3 wires is enough to handle a 450WATT load safely. The fourth wire in this case thus gives us 13.5 AMP of safe headroom. NOW in safe I mean quite conservative and is for 1m of cable run in this case. In this short of a run 16 AWG can carry up to 22 AMP without exceeding 70C. which means that 2 wires could carry the 450WATT load (not recommended but shouldn't present danger). The soldering and the bus bar for better word because its only a few millimeters will easily handle the load even with the solder because its such a short relative distance and the resistance would be very low so those joints when PROPERLY contacting and secured would also be relatively safe even at full load. (Hence they could run 1400 WATT  at 12V through the connector before it started melting as at 1400WATT its 117 AMPS and 29AMPS per wire on 4 wires definitely over my theoretical safe 22 AMP draw per wire hence melting at this point through as simply too much amperage at 12V for 16 AWG).  The problem for failure would thus have to be from the solder joints breaking off but now having an air gap, which leads to a massive resistance jump and arcing. Once the wires become separated enough on the ends it would be fine again once they break contact completely as I went over in most cases or unless you live in the Sahara Dessert with no AC. 2 Wires would still be able handle the full 450WATT load if the solder joints remain firm.   Definitely the solder cracking and not using a direct pinned design is at the heart of this issue. My math should support this.

The problem with heat is not to completely sever the connection, but to increase the resistance. Imagine the solder breaking, but the cable still touching the pin. This will increase the resistance, but will still allow flow through. If the draw is distributed equally through all the pins, the bad connection will have more resistance and have increased heat.

From my experience using a thermal camera, when a wire makes a bad contact and high amounts of current runs through it, this will create lots of heat like a welder. So my assumption is that when the wire breaks from the stress, it doesn't disconnect completely, and that barely touching cable is enough to cause much heat to melt the connector. You disconnected the broken wire completely, effectively removing that resistance, so you got no heat. The remaining cables that are properly connected will not heat up so much just because a higher load gets through them. Those wires have multiple cores inside, just cut most of the cores from a single wire and leave just 1-2 cores and then they will definitely start burning.

Jay, you‚Äôre close on the constituent component level of understanding the problem. You‚Äôre thinking of power only, but contact resistance and subsequent heat isn‚Äôt from power, it‚Äôs from current. Just like you‚Äôre used to looking at CPU & GPU overclocking from the voltage perspective, terminals and terminal blocks care about current.  Heat generated from a contact is linearly proportional to resistance, but is proportional to the square of current. So, when you remove 25% of the current carrying conductors, the loss from current goes up by ~77.8%. If the loss of power contacts is from loosing a pin, that translates to ~122% more power loss. All power loss is via heat.

While this is clearly an issue, I'm not convinced this is directly related to the connectors showing melted pins. The solder joints are located near the rear of the connector and the melting is occurring at the connector's pins. If you have a bad solder joint and you're pulling power through it, you create localised heat where that solder joint is through increased resistance via lower contact area. But due to the bus design (assuming that the foil connecting the pins is still intact), the power past this point is still distributed across all the pins which wouldn't cause overheating at the connection joint. I'm convinced that Galax's interpretation is that the pin contact isn't always good, so the connectors are melting at the pins due to this. The only way I could foresee the disconnected solder joins being this issue is if the easiest way for the heat to escape is along the path of the connector towards the GPU, but that would have to be a significant amount of plastic around the solder joints if there is no signs of melting around that area.  If you're looking to recreate the issue Igor is describing, you need to break the connections on the outer wires and then loosely reconnect them with a very small contact area (basically leave them dangling onto the pin they're designed to connect to...).

you got it all wrong, it's not the severed connection that creates the overheating due to resistance, it is a weak/loose or poor contact that creates the overheating. The smaller the contact point, the hotter the contact point will get if the same amount of power is going through the contact point. The outer connectors tends to overheat and melt because as the wires are bent sideways, the contact (solder joints) may partially break, but because they are still inside the molder plastic, the connection isn't complete severed, but instead now the contact is weak/loose or less, or having slight cap, which will result in overheating. Same with a connection that is not seated fully or straight, it creates a scenario where you now have less contact surface or weaker contact and the less contact will result in overheating.
This is what happens when you try to save pennies.

Here is a little electrics 101 The problem is not with wires broken completely off. The problem comes when a solder joint cracks or breaks off, but touches slightly. A bad connection will produce much more heat, compared to if it disconnected completely and forcing the other cables to carry the full load. If the card draws 615 watt at 12 volts, it's drawing 51.25 amps. That devided over the 4 wires is 12.8 amps pr. wire, and the wires look like they are what, at least 10 AWG and are rated for 35 amps each. Even if you broke off 2 wires leaving you with only 2 connected ones, each will only draw 25.6 amps, and they are rated for 35 amps, so no problems there. The distribution plate is thin yes, but also has a large cross section, so no problem there. The problem comes from bad connections in broken solder joints. To recreate the problem, you must break off a solder joint, and just lay the wire on the connection barely touching. Then you will see heat. Hope that makes sence.  Keep up the good work Jay.

I have NO idea what possessed Nvidia to make the insane 12 pin connector/adaptor to 4 x 6 pin PCIe connector assembly but whatever, they've made several GROSS engineering errors in the process.  The first, which was bothering me, was that the number of pins didn't match the number of wires, so they were doing something 'dodgy' or unapproved in the construction of it. I wondered if they were crimping more than one wire into a single female crimp terminal, which is distinctly 'amateur'.  Tearing it apart revealed something even WORSE. Those soldered wires aren't even STRESS RELIEVED. Why is that important ? Well. the crimp terminals thatrely on a high pressure crimp termination ( no soldering) *** ALSO have a stress relief built into them ***. The crimping tool deforms 2 parts of the contact. 1. the wire termination and 2, a stress relief that grips on the *INSULATION* of the wire, not the actual copper wire. THIS IS VERY IMPORTANT !  Because thar assembly has NO (proper) STRESS RELIEF, that's exactly how wires can be bent and SEVERED (broken) ! The load current then has to go through the other remaining pins remaining, increasing the AMPs per power pin and increasing the heat generated by *THE SQUARE* of that increased current, i.e. DOUBLY *BAD*.  Frankly IMHO if Underwriters Labs looked at that 12 pin 'dooh-dah' they'd bin any approval it may have gained (unlikely actually) on the basis it'll probably FAIL in service and be a likely fire hazard.  FYI, the copper foil used in circuit boards is normally 35 or 70 microns (micrometres) thick (industry standards). If the width of that trace/track is 5mm then it's equivalent to a wire gauge of only 0.35mm2 which is **** 22 AWG *** at BEST ! Seriously do Nvidia want to draw 40+ Amps through 22 AWG ? OK, the load is spread out and there's solder on top which conducts too but *** SERIOUSLY *** ?  Nvidia have BADLY DROPPED THE BALL here and a redesign from the ground up that properly handle a LOT of current is *required*. There can be no saving face by the idiot management. It needs to be fixed and fixed right NOW !  To be honest, this just looks like an amateurish BODGE to me. I suspect there may be a legal action waiting to go if they don't fix it too. Nvidia have now been TOLD that their engineering (I use the term advisedly) STINKS !  FYI, I looked up the specs of those Molex crimp terminals and one thing that stood out is that their current rating PLUMMETS when you use a wire of less than 16 AWG. They *KNOW* that the terminals run hot and are RELYING ON THE WIRE TO REMOVE HEAT ! The terminal are rated at around ONLY 7 Amps per pin when used with 16 AWG but it rapidly drops to ONE Amp with 20 AWG. You really have to inspect product specifications like a HAWK for this kind of thing. I'm sure plenty of cheap Chinese PCIe power leads skimp on the wire and thus reduce the curernt/power rating of the connector.  Just a 68 year old, very experienced Consultant Engineer here. 'Kids' typically don't or can't grasp this level of detail sadly, which is exactly how Nvidia screwed up.

Ohm's law... partially broken connections get hot due to high resistance. Power=I^2*R. Fully broken connections just share the load across the remaining wires with more current per wire. With 4 wires, 500w through 4 wires is ~10A per wire, plenty for safety. 500W through 3 wires in the broken wire case is ~14A, also reasonable. Now for a single partial break with 0.1 of an ohm resistance in any wire, as much as a watt could be dissipated just in the resistance of the terminal - enough heat to melt plastic. Solution is better connectors, better strain relief.

The problem is you're not creating an arc gap.  You have to have the break, but leave it close enough to arc.  When stuck in the connector, it's going to be a 1mm gap, if even that.  An arc at the lowest temperature it's possible to generate one is going to be about 1500 to 2000 degrees.  It's just when it's small (literally the width of a few electrons) everything around the point of impact/arc bridge can absorb the heat.  However, when it's a prolonged exposure that adds up real fast.  Additionally, you don't even need a clean break that would cause an arc. Close enough to pass electricty and not create an arc generates quite a bit of heat as well.  That's what causes your CPU to get so warm via the contact points on the LGA.  Think of this phenomenon like adding a heatsink with no fans or thermal paste to a CPU, and without overtemp protection.  Sucker is going to get hot.  It may take awhile depending on how good your contact plates interface, but it will eventually get there.

To recreate the problem instead of breaking of the wire completely you should probably leave it loosely touching the solder point. That should generate much more heat. As you mentioned in the video, the solder pad of Igor's connector broke off after he pulled the rubber off. Before that there was still some loose contact between the wire and the pad. That loose connection probably caused some sparks to occur and it generated enough heat to melt the plastic.   Arcing When electricity jumps across a gap between two conductors and creates a spark, the phenomenon is called arcing. As the electricity ionizes the air to produce the spark, it produces heat at the same time. Arcing near an electric outlet can create enough heat to melt the plastic. It can be caused by a loose connection on one of the hot terminal screws, by a poor connection inside the outlet or by a loose clamp that doesn't hold the plug securely enough. Outlet clamps wear out, given enough time, or they can be loosened when plugs are pulled out often and carelessly.
Part of the problem is you "cut" the wire. What you should do is break the tab, but leave it in the connector. There may also be arcing going on that is causing the heat increase and "melting" due to the broken solder tab still being in the connector.

your explanation doesnt make sense. if the end wires are the ones breaking and the full load is now going to only the inner pins then the inner pins would be the ones heating up. if the outer pins are broken off and disconnected then it would be impossible to heat them up. A disconnected wire has zero resistance and there are no electrons flowing. there is zero heating up of those pins because they are disconnected. If pins are heating up to the point of melting plastic then there is either a very slight connection of the broken solder joint making all that current flow through something with huge resistance and nowhere near the guage to handle the current, or power to ground is being shorted within the plug, or something like that. You also keep referring to the watts as being the culprit. Watts is just volts times amps. The watts could be the same number and be perfectly fine or be melting shit depending on the current. The current is what matters. if these pins are all 12v and the card will see 450W max then thats 450/12=37.5A. 14 guage wire can handle 15A max. and if there's 4 of them then 37.5/4=9.375A each.. If two of the wires disconnect forcing all the load onto 2 wires then its 37.5/2=18.75A each. Which is above the 15A maximum. So it would be the connected wires that are being overloaded and melting. So if the outer wires are the ones melting then i would assume its the outer wires that are remaining connected while the inner wires are being disconnected.. Which is the opposite of what youre saying is going on... you're wrong and igor's lab isn't wrong but not totally right either. the issue IS the connection side not an input wire coming lose (though it is related to the fact there are only a few power wires coming in instead of 1 wire per pin). It's the connector side that goes into plug that is getting hot and melting not the wire side connecting to the connector itself. If you want to reproduce the issue then plug in the connector but not all the way and put tension length wise on the connector so that the side pin is more into the connector than the far side. This create geometry where the internal pins have a point contact with the pins instead of over a bigger area which will cause the heating up of those specific pins. the reason the inner pins don't overheat is because 1 input wire of power is being spread out over 2 pins compared to the outer pins where 1 input wire is only going through 1 pin. The issue will not occur if there is a wire per pin or if the edge pins maintain connection to the inner pins. The issue specifically occurs when the edge pins are not connected to the inner pins and those pins have to deliver more power than the other pins (again some of them have 1 wire per 2 pins and those won't get as hot) combined with not well seated connector which changes the connection to a point contact internally. Combine those 3 factors and you have a melting connection.
Jay, you're almost correct. The issue is indeed the broken solder joint, but it's not the added current load on the remaining wires.   The issue with the broken solder connection is that it forms what is know as a "High Resistance Connection". While the solder joint is no longer solid, both sides of the broken joint are still conductors and will conduct if they are in physical contact. However, if there is only a small amount of pressure, it will create a poor connection, with resistance measuring in the hundreds of milliohms upwards to multiple ohms. And P=I^2R.  That's the reason why your test with cutting the wires didn't recreate the issue.

So, one thing to keep in mind is thermal expansion. Having good airflow in a case means that adapter will cool down fast. If the solder points cool down too fast too often, they will start to gain micro cracks. More cracks, more resistance. More Resistance, more heat. Sorry, but no.  melting is localised to the point of resistance,  for the plastic to be melted at the "key" it would be localised at the key, not the "foil".  a crack at the foil would cause the heat to be located in the base of the connector.  the only way for these pins to be melting these "keys" is for the pins themselves to be high resistance, from not being pushed in securely enough or split female.
If the side connection is completely severed, how could that connection be the one that melts? It‚Äôs going to have no current going through it at all, the other connections are where the extra current and therefore heat should be. It‚Äôs when the side connector is only partially disconnected that you‚Äôll have a problem, as the same current will be trying to go through a small area.

In industrial electrical systems, it's not permitted* to run multiple parallel wires for high-current applications for exactly this reason.  You're required to run bigger cables, not more of the same size.   I also don't get why these big power components have to use these odd connectors.  Use screw terminals and twin 6-gage cables! Put a thematically-designed cover on the connection, and it would look awesome! And never catch fire.  *- prohibited from running parallel conductors below 00 gage cable.  Really big stuff permits parallel power cables because there's no other way.  But in the sub 2-gage cable world? No.
The problem is not the temperature, but the minimal gap that occurs when the cable breaks off in the housing, depending on the humidity, a spark bridge can occur, these sparks have when they bridge the gap they generate plasma (like in "electric lighters" to ignite the gas or with spark plugs) and plasma is known to have a temperature of up to 30,000 degrees celsius. the heat cannot be dissipated due to the insulating effect of the plastic  and therefore the housing melts that is, for example, one reason why spark plugs are insulated with porcelain and not with plastic

The reason the solder looks worse in igors is because the higher current on the wires creates enough heat to also over heat the solder. You can only heat solder so hot much or so many times. What you see in his is "dead solder".
Jay the problem is not a wire being cut off. Arcing from a broken solder point but touching wire is what causes heat to rise and plastic to melt. Same way it happens to electrical sockets when something arks when you plug or unplug it powered on!
The problem i have with everyone saying the problem is where the solder joints is causing the melting the pins,to me is incorrect, being an auto electrician of 25+ years I have seen many melted cables and plugs, The melted connector section is where the problem is, and with out having a plug to test/check i cant say for sure. If it was a broken solder joint and its arcing then melted  point will be where the arcing is not down the cable/pin, cutting cable will not cause high temps further down cable/pin. electricity doesn't work like that. No connection, no flow, close contact (broken solder joint) =possible arcing makes heat = melted cables/plastic housing in that spot, not at pins.  It to me looks like pin contact area is the problem, that's where the heat will build up and cause possible melting,  If there is to much power draw being used temps will rise where ever there is poor connection, thinnest wire, in case of a connector the contact points,  The gpu has a solid pin (Male side), so thats not the issues, plug is hollow (Female side) that is the issue, Surface contact area.

I think you would call that part like a mini-distributor block/bridge or something along those lines. In electronics there are often times copper pieces that tie together multiple connectors and route the power flow to single connectors or vice versa. They act as a bridge. The thing is, I almost never see those in connectors designed for large current. Usually, when current becomes higher, they are located INSIDE the electronic device. I'm not an engineer or an expert. I simply take shit apart a lot and 90% fail to put it back together. So, take what I say with a grain of salt.

It's a good theory but no dice as far as im concerned. the amount of molded rubberized casing around it plus all the other stuff makes it unlikely the solder joint would crack You can see how much the rubber is gripping the wires by the fact that the wire sheeathing looks basically crimped. Also electricity follows the path of leas resistance so if what you were saying is true most ofthe current would flow through the center pins and so the outer pins would not be melting. not to mention what you are seeing is six pins without really any deliberate bridging with the wires being soldered to multiple pins its basically the uncrimped connector with the wires soldered instead of crimped so this does not explain the pictures. The way this would explain it is if this happened in conjunction with improperly seated middle connectors or all of them failed but the outer ones were making more contact than the center ones so the path of least resistance was on the outside but even then you would notice other stability problems due to dirty power from this.  Occam's razer. Which is more likely a loosely seated pcie connector or the outer pad broke but instead of taking the path of least resistance it somehow decided to go across a path with more resistance. This is  a problem maybe but if this happened you wouldn't see the failure that occured in the pictures which your own experimentation shows. all the pcitures of failed connectors shows that the outer pins are heating up. What these picture show is the inner wires feed to pins each and the outer pins feed one pin each. So i guarantee if the card thinks it can draw 600 watts but the center pins are disconnected you would see the problem like in the pictures but on the other hand if the outer pins were disconnected i imagine because it's spread over 4 pins instead of 2 pins it would be still warm but not as warm. So, to recreate the failure in a 'natural' way, you most likely: 1. need a badly soldered plug 2. need to have wiggled it side to side so that at least 1 (and probably 2) sides aren't making proper connection 3. need to have the plug not plugged in deeply enough.  That 3rd step is probably why you still don't but your card, because those numbers that you showed immediately went from 70 to 100+ c when incorrectly plugged in.  It might even be that the 3rd part is the most important one, because you haven't found shocking numbers with your correctly seated cables.

Solder joins are notorious for this kind of thing in the automotive industry which is why we rely on crimp joins instead. Solder creates a hard point which can crack or snap with movement and can wick up the copper conductor as well as cause heat stress points. Crimps are purely mechanical...  I think we are seeing a temporary or slight connection issue where the solder is broken just enough to create a resistance across the solder join and foil. This would cause a massive amount of heat. That doesn't make sense though. The location of the heat should be the opposite of what he said. If the connections break at the edges, then the heat should be coming from the middle. From the parts that are still connected. So if you see melting near one pin, it should mean that all of the other pins are not connected. Or the problem comes from the graphics card side. If the Card isn't drawing power equally from all pins, then select ones can heat up.
I'd call the "foil metal" a "bus bar" or as they're usually named here in the UK... A "buzz bar" because that's what your insides will do if you touch one
Wouldn't you still need the broken connector to be free in the connector to recreate it? A combination of the broken wire creating an intermittent short and an increased load draw on the remaining cables. I can't see there being damage on only the broken pin/pins and not the others if it's purely a heat/over draw issue. It would damage the connector all around. Maybe it cracked but didn‚Äôt disconnect fully? I imagine that‚Äôd get pretty hot since you‚Äôre effectively reducing the wire gauge/raising resistance. Bad soldering (or even just old joints) are super prone to cracking.   I repair old parts sometimes, and I can‚Äôt tell you how many times the fault was in a connection having an invisible crack. I‚Äôm automatically sus of any joint that isn‚Äôt shiny and smooth, or one that‚Äôs in a part that gets really hot and cools down a lot over use. Cold joints (rough looking solder that didn‚Äôt fully melt when put together) or mechanically damaged one‚Äôs crack super easily. Same with joints that were done on a dirty surface and don‚Äôt wet properly.   I don‚Äôt know how relevant that is to industrial scale, but I can see any of these happening in a factory trying to cram out connectors as fast as they can for as cheap as possible‚Ä¶
Soldering pins like this will very often create problems because the solder will wick up the stranded wire. This makes things significantly less flexible and bending forces are more likely to cause breaks/cracks in the wire and solder. For situations that need any flexi should only be crimped.

They should have re-designed the adapter so that the distribution block is separate from the connector, and had separate wires going into each pin. That way there is no risk of this ever happening again. Will a solution like this look uglier? Yes and no! Will it be safer in operation? Yes! Will there be more melted plastic? No!  It is completely possible to make it so that all the +12v and -12v wires are on separate bus-bars in the middle of the cable, with the corresponding 6+6 wires going out to each respective pin on the connector. With a solution like this, you can make the wire going into the connector circular. It should also be possible to make the adapter cable longer, so that the bus bar section can be hidden under a PSU shroud (by also running thicker gauge wire from the bus bars)

The term you're looking for is dry joint - dry joints are not only created by bad soldering / welding where oxidation or soldering / welding flux got captured, but also by mechanical stress that can create a crack promoting oxidation resulting in decreased impedance. In power industry it was long known that soldering high current joints is a bad idea ... a bit surprising why standardising body for this cable even allowed this design.

So has everyone left out another VERY important factor? 16ga wire, Is designed for upto 13Amps of current.... X4 wires, that means, That connector is safely designed for upto 52A...  That card is capable of drawing 600w?!?!    12v 600w = 50Amps.... And your meant to use a cable that can handle upto 10% more amperage, for "headroom" reasons... 50A draw on a connector thats designed for 52A, Is pushing its boundaries a bit... Especially when the GPU can technically draw digits in excess of 600w..... Cutting it awfully close to its maximum rated current specs. and most definately isnt allowing for atleast 10% headroom, to keep the wires cool...

Crimping is always more reliable than a soldered connection. A soldered connection always introduces a weak point because of thermal meltdown. A clamped connected doesn't come apart. When I see automotive soldering I cringe.
the old robust 8-pin adapter is 3 power + 3 ground + 2 sense for a total of 150W, that is 50W per power-/ground-pin. the new flaky 16-pin connector is 6 power + 6 ground + 4 sense for a total of 600W, that is 100W per power-/ground-pin and the new pins are tiny compared to the old robust 8-pin adapter. there will be problems with the new adapter no matter how well they try making it, the pins are just too small and too few! the new 16-pin adapter is good for maybe 150W (25W per tiny power-pin).
